They were, in increasing order from Earth (in Ptolemy's order and using modern names): the Moon, Mercury, Venus, the Sun, Mars, Jupiter, and Saturn.[[159]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-oed-160)[[174]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-almagest-175)[[175]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-176)

### Medieval astronomy

[![Image 16](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/1660_illustration_of_Claudius_Ptolemy%27s_geocentric_model_of_the_Universe.jpg/500px-1660_illustration_of_Claudius_Ptolemy%27s_geocentric_model_of_the_Universe.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:1660_illustration_of_Claudius_Ptolemy%27s_geocentric_model_of_the_Universe.jpg)

1660 illustration of Claudius Ptolemy's geocentric model

After the [fall of the Western Roman Empire](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fall_of_the_Western_Roman_Empire "Fall of the Western Roman Empire"), astronomy developed further in India and the medieval Islamic world. In 499 CE, the Indian astronomer [Aryabhata](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aryabhata "Aryabhata") propounded a planetary model that explicitly incorporated [Earth's rotation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth%27s_rotation "Earth's rotation") about its axis, which he explains as the cause of what appears to be an apparent westward motion of the stars. He also theorized that the orbits of planets were [elliptical](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ellipse "Ellipse").[[176]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-177) Aryabhata's followers were particularly strong in [South India](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/South_India "South India"), where his principles of the diurnal rotation of Earth, among others, were followed and a number of secondary works were based on them.[[177]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-178)

The astronomy of the [Islamic Golden Age](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Islamic_Golden_Age "Islamic Golden Age") mostly took place in the [Middle East](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Middle_East "Middle East"), [Central Asia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Central_Asia "Central Asia"), [Al-Andalus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Al-Andalus "Al-Andalus"), and [North Africa](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/North_Africa "North Africa"), and later in the [Far East](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Far_East "Far East") and India. These astronomers, like the polymath [Ibn al-Haytham](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ibn_al-Haytham "Ibn al-Haytham"), generally accepted geocentrism, although they did dispute Ptolemy's system of epicycles and sought alternatives. The 10th-century astronomer [Abu Sa'id al-Sijzi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abu_Sa%27id_al-Sijzi "Abu Sa'id al-Sijzi") accepted that the Earth rotates around its axis.[[178]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-Scientia-179) In the 11th century, the [transit of Venus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Transit_of_Venus "Transit of Venus") was observed by [Avicenna](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Avicenna "Avicenna").[[179]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-180) His contemporary [Al-Biruni](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Al-Biruni "Al-Biruni") devised a method of determining the Earth's radius using [trigonometry](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Trigonometry "Trigonometry") that, unlike the older method of [Eratosthenes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eratosthenes "Eratosthenes"), only required observations at a single mountain.[[180]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-The_Lost_Art_of_Finding_Our_Way-181)

### Scientific Revolution and discovery of outer planets

[![Image 17](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/The_Solar_System%2C_with_the_orbits_of_5_remarkable_comets._LOC_2013593161.jpg/250px-The_Solar_System%2C_with_the_orbits_of_5_remarkable_comets._LOC_2013593161.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:The_Solar_System,_with_the_orbits_of_5_remarkable_comets._LOC_2013593161.jpg)

True-scale Solar System poster made by [Emanuel Bowen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Emanuel_Bowen "Emanuel Bowen") in 1747. At that time, Uranus, Neptune, and the asteroid belts had all not yet been discovered.

With the advent of the [Scientific Revolution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scientific_Revolution "Scientific Revolution") and the [heliocentric model](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heliocentric_model "Heliocentric model") of [Copernicus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Copernicus "Copernicus"), [Galileo](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Galileo "Galileo"), and [Kepler](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kepler "Kepler"), use of the term "planet" changed from something that moved around the sky relative to the [fixed star](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fixed_star "Fixed star") to a body that orbited the Sun, directly (a primary planet) or indirectly (a secondary or satellite planet). Thus the Earth was added to the roster of planets,[[181]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-galileo_project-182) and the Sun was removed. The Copernican count of primary planets stood until 1781, when [William Herschel](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/William_Herschel "William Herschel") discovered [Uranus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Uranus "Uranus").[[182]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-Dreyer-183)

When four satellites of Jupiter (the [Galilean moons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Galilean_moons "Galilean moons")) and five of Saturn were discovered in the 17th century, they joined Earth's Moon in the category of "satellite planets" or "secondary planets" orbiting the primary planets, though in the following decades they would come to be called simply "satellites" for short. Scientists generally considered planetary satellites to also be planets until about the 1920s, although this usage was not common among non-scientists.[[154]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-metzger22-155)

In the first decade of the 19th century, four new 'planets' were discovered: [Ceres](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ceres_(dwarf_planet) "Ceres (dwarf planet)") (in 1801), [Pallas](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2_Pallas "2 Pallas") (in 1802), [Juno](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/3_Juno "3 Juno") (in 1804), and [Vesta](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/4_Vesta "4 Vesta") (in 1807). It soon became apparent that they were rather different from previously known planets: they shared the same general region of space, between Mars and Jupiter (the [asteroid belt](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Asteroid_belt "Asteroid belt")), with sometimes overlapping orbits. This was an area where only one planet had been expected, and they were much smaller than all other planets; indeed, it was suspected that they might be shards of a larger planet that had broken up. Herschel called them _[asteroids](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Asteroid "Asteroid")_ (from the Greek for "starlike") because even in the largest telescopes they resembled stars, without a resolvable disk.[[153]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-asteroids-154)[[183]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-184)

The situation was stable for four decades, but in the 1840s several additional asteroids were discovered ([Astraea](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/5_Astraea "5 Astraea") in 1845; [Hebe](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/6_Hebe "6 Hebe"), [Iris](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/7_Iris "7 Iris"), and [Flora](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/8_Flora "8 Flora") in 1847; [Metis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/9_Metis "9 Metis") in 1848; and [Hygiea](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/10_Hygiea "10 Hygiea") in 1849). New "planets" were discovered every year; as a result, astronomers began tabulating the asteroids ([minor planets](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Minor_planet "Minor planet")) separately from the major planets and assigning them numbers instead of abstract [planetary symbols](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planetary_symbol "Planetary symbol"),[[153]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-asteroids-154) although they continued to be considered as small planets.[[184]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-metzger19-185)

[Neptune was discovered in 1846](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Discovery_of_Neptune "Discovery of Neptune"), its position having been predicted thanks to its gravitational influence upon Uranus. Because the orbit of Mercury appeared to be affected in a similar way, it was believed in the late 19th century that there might be [another planet even closer to the Sun](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vulcan_(hypothetical_planet) "Vulcan (hypothetical planet)"). However, the discrepancy between Mercury's orbit and the predictions of Newtonian gravity was instead explained by an improved theory of gravity, Einstein's [general relativity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/General_relativity "General relativity").[[185]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-186)[[186]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-187)

[Pluto](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pluto "Pluto") was discovered in 1930. After initial observations led to the belief that it was larger than Earth,[[187]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-188) the object was immediately accepted as the ninth major planet. Further monitoring found the body was actually much smaller: in 1936, [Ray Lyttleton](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Raymond_Arthur_Lyttleton "Raymond Arthur Lyttleton") suggested that Pluto may be an escaped satellite of [Neptune](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neptune "Neptune"),[[188]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-189) and [Fred Whipple](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fred_Lawrence_Whipple "Fred Lawrence Whipple") suggested in 1964 that Pluto may be a comet.[[189]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-190) The discovery of its large moon [Charon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Charon_(moon) "Charon (moon)") in 1978 showed that Pluto was only 0.2% the mass of Earth.[[190]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-ChristyHarrington1978-191) As this was still substantially more massive than any known asteroid, and because no other [trans-Neptunian objects](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Trans-Neptunian_objects "Trans-Neptunian objects") had been discovered at that time, Pluto kept its planetary status, only officially losing it in 2006.[[191]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-192)[[192]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-Pluto_loses_status_as_a_planet-2006-193)

In the 1950s, [Gerard Kuiper](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gerard_Kuiper "Gerard Kuiper") published papers on the origin of the asteroids. He recognized that asteroids were typically not spherical, as had previously been thought, and that the [asteroid families](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Asteroid_family "Asteroid family") were remnants of collisions. Thus he differentiated between the largest asteroids as "true planets" versus the smaller ones as collisional fragments. From the 1960s onwards, the term "minor planet" was mostly displaced by the term "asteroid", and references to the asteroids as planets in the literature became scarce, except for the geologically evolved largest three: Ceres, and less often Pallas and Vesta.[[184]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-metzger19-185)

The beginning of Solar System exploration by space probes in the 1960s spurred a renewed interest in planetary science. A split in definitions regarding satellites occurred around then: planetary scientists began to reconsider the large moons as also being planets, but astronomers who were not planetary scientists generally did not.[[154]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-metzger22-155) (This is not exactly the same as the definition used in the previous century, which classed _all_ satellites as secondary planets, even non-round ones like Saturn's [Hyperion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hyperion_(moon) "Hyperion (moon)") or Mars's [Phobos](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Phobos_(moon) "Phobos (moon)") and [Deimos](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Deimos_(moon) "Deimos (moon)").)[[193]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-194)[[194]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-195) All the eight major planets and their planetary-mass moons have since been explored by spacecraft, as have many asteroids and the dwarf planets Ceres and Pluto; however, so far the only planetary-mass body beyond Earth that has been explored by humans is the Moon.[[b]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-196)

### Defining the term _planet_

A growing number of astronomers argued for Pluto to be declassified as a planet, because many similar objects approaching its size had been found in the same region of the Solar System (the [Kuiper belt](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kuiper_belt "Kuiper belt")) during the 1990s and early 2000s. Pluto was found to be just one "small" body in a population of thousands.[[195]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-plutoplanet-197) They often referred to the demotion of the asteroids as a precedent, although that had been done based on their geophysical differences from planets rather than their being in a belt.[[154]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-metzger22-155) Some of the larger [trans-Neptunian objects](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Trans-Neptunian_object "Trans-Neptunian object"), such as [Quaoar](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/50000_Quaoar "50000 Quaoar"), [Sedna](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/90377_Sedna "90377 Sedna"), [Eris](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eris_(dwarf_planet) "Eris (dwarf planet)"), and [Haumea](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Haumea "Haumea"),[[196]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-planeta-198) were heralded in the popular press as the [tenth planet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tenth_planet "Tenth planet").

The announcement of Eris in 2005, an object 27% more massive than Pluto, created the impetus for an official definition of a planet,[[195]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-plutoplanet-197) as considering Pluto a planet would logically have demanded that Eris be considered a planet as well. Since different procedures were in place for naming planets versus non-planets, this created an urgent situation because under the rules Eris could not be named without defining what a planet was.[[154]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-metzger22-155) At the time, it was also thought that the size required for a trans-Neptunian object to become round was about the same as that required for the moons of the giant planets (about 400 km diameter), a figure that would have suggested about 200 round objects in the Kuiper belt and thousands more beyond.[[197]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-Brown-199)[[198]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-BrownList-200) Many astronomers argued that the public would not accept a definition creating a large number of planets.[[154]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-metzger22-155)

To acknowledge the problem, the [International Astronomical Union](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Astronomical_Union "International Astronomical Union") (IAU) set about creating the [definition of planet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/IAU_definition_of_planet "IAU definition of planet") and produced one in August 2006. Under this definition, the Solar System is considered to have eight planets (Mercury, Venus, Earth, Mars, Jupiter, Saturn, Uranus, and Neptune). Bodies that fulfill the first two conditions but not the third are classified as [dwarf planets](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dwarf_planet "Dwarf planet"), provided they are not [natural satellites](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Natural_satellite "Natural satellite") of other planets. Originally an IAU committee had proposed a definition that would have included a larger number of planets as it did not include (c) as a criterion.[[199]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-201) After much discussion, it was decided via a vote that those bodies should instead be classified as dwarf planets.[[192]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-Pluto_loses_status_as_a_planet-2006-193)[[200]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-202)

#### Criticisms and alternatives to IAU definition

[![Image 18](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/25_solar_system_objects_smaller_than_Earth.jpg/500px-25_solar_system_objects_smaller_than_Earth.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:25_solar_system_objects_smaller_than_Earth.jpg)

The planetary-mass moons to scale, compared with Mercury, Venus, Earth, Mars, and Pluto. Sub-planetary [Proteus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Proteus_(moon) "Proteus (moon)") and [Nereid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nereid_(moon) "Nereid (moon)") (about the same size as Mimas) have been included for comparison. Unimaged [Dysnomia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dysnomia_(moon) "Dysnomia (moon)") (intermediate in size between Tethys and Enceladus) is not shown; it is in any case probably not a solid body.[[90]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-Brown2023-91)

The IAU definition has not been universally used or accepted. In [planetary geology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planetary_geology "Planetary geology"), celestial objects are [defined as planets by geophysical characteristics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geophysical_definition_of_planet "Geophysical definition of planet"). A celestial body may acquire a dynamic (planetary) geology at approximately the mass required for its mantle to become plastic under its own weight. This leads to a state of [hydrostatic equilibrium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hydrostatic_equilibrium "Hydrostatic equilibrium") where the body acquires a stable, round shape, which is adopted as the hallmark of planethood by geophysical definitions. For example:[[201]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-Stern_Levison_2002-203)

> a substellar-mass body that has never undergone nuclear fusion and has enough gravitation to be round due to hydrostatic equilibrium, regardless of its orbital parameters.[[202]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-Astronomy-204)

In the Solar System, this mass is generally less than the mass required for a body to clear its orbit; thus, some objects that are considered "planets" under geophysical definitions are not considered as such under the IAU definition, such as Ceres and Pluto.[[4]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-planetarysociety-4) (In practice, the requirement for hydrostatic equilibrium is universally relaxed to a requirement for rounding and compaction under self-gravity; Mercury is not actually in hydrostatic equilibrium,[[203]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-Mercury-205) but is universally included as a planet regardless.)[[204]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-206) Proponents of such definitions often argue that location should not matter and that planethood should be defined by the intrinsic properties of an object.[[4]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-planetarysociety-4) Dwarf planets had been proposed as a category of small planet (as opposed to [planetoids](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Minor_planet "Minor planet") as sub-planetary objects) and planetary geologists continue to treat them as planets despite the IAU definition.[[32]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-Grundy2019-32)

The number of dwarf planets even among known objects is not certain. In 2019, Grundy et al. argued based on the low densities of some mid-sized trans-Neptunian objects that the limiting size required for a trans-Neptunian object to reach equilibrium was in fact much larger than it is for the icy moons of the giant planets, being about 900–1000 km diameter.[[32]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-Grundy2019-32) There is general consensus on Ceres in the asteroid belt[[205]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-207) and on the eight trans-Neptunians that probably cross this threshold—[Orcus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orcus_(dwarf_planet) "Orcus (dwarf planet)"), [Pluto](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pluto "Pluto"), [Haumea](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Haumea "Haumea"), [Quaoar](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quaoar "Quaoar"), [Makemake](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Makemake "Makemake"), [Gonggong](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gonggong_(dwarf_planet) "Gonggong (dwarf planet)"), [Eris](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eris_(dwarf_planet) "Eris (dwarf planet)"), and [Sedna](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sedna_(dwarf_planet) "Sedna (dwarf planet)").[[206]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-208)[[33]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-JWST-33)

Planetary geologists may include the nineteen known [planetary-mass moons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planetary-mass_moon "Planetary-mass moon") as "satellite planets", including Earth's Moon and Pluto's [Charon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Charon_(moon) "Charon (moon)"), like the early modern astronomers.[[4]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-planetarysociety-4)[[207]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-News.discovery.com-209) Some go even further and include as planets relatively large, geologically evolved bodies that are nonetheless not very round today, such as Pallas and Vesta;[[4]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-planetarysociety-4) rounded bodies that were completely disrupted by impacts and re-accreted like Hygiea;[[208]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-210)[[209]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-211)[[110]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-Yang2020-111) or even everything at least the diameter of Saturn's moon [Mimas](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mimas_(moon) "Mimas (moon)"), the smallest planetary-mass moon. (This may even include objects that are not round but happen to be larger than Mimas, like Neptune's moon [Proteus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Proteus_(moon) "Proteus (moon)").)[[4]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-planetarysociety-4)

Astronomer [Jean-Luc Margot](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jean-Luc_Margot "Jean-Luc Margot") proposed a mathematical criterion that determines whether an object can clear its orbit during the lifetime of its host star, based on the mass of the planet, its semimajor axis, and the mass of its host star.[[210]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-212) The formula produces a value called π that is greater than 1 for planets.[[c]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-not-confuse-%CF%80-214) The eight known planets and all known exoplanets have π values above 100, while Ceres, Pluto, and Eris have π values of 0.1, or less. Objects with π values of 1 or more are expected to be approximately spherical, so that objects that fulfill the orbital-zone clearance requirement around Sun-like stars will also fulfill the roundness requirement[[211]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-Margot-213) – though this may not be the case around very low-mass stars.[[212]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-Margot_2024-215) In 2024, Margot and collaborators proposed a revised version of the criterion with a uniform clearing timescale of 10 billion years (the approximate main-sequence lifetime of the Sun) or 13.8 billion years (the [age of the universe](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Age_of_the_universe "Age of the universe")) to accommodate planets orbiting brown dwarfs.[[212]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-Margot_2024-215)

### Exoplanets

Even before the discovery of [exoplanets](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Exoplanet "Exoplanet"), there were particular disagreements over whether an object should be considered a planet if it was part of a distinct population such as a [belt](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Asteroid_belt "Asteroid belt"), or if it was large enough to generate energy by the [thermonuclear fusion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thermonuclear_fusion "Thermonuclear fusion") of [deuterium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Deuterium "Deuterium").[[195]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-plutoplanet-197) Complicating the matter even further, bodies too small to generate energy by fusing deuterium can form by [gas-cloud](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nebula "Nebula") collapse just like stars and brown dwarfs, even down to the mass of Jupiter:[[213]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-216) there was thus disagreement about whether how a body formed should be taken into account.[[195]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-plutoplanet-197)

In 1992, astronomers [Aleksander Wolszczan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aleksander_Wolszczan "Aleksander Wolszczan") and [Dale Frail](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dale_Frail "Dale Frail") announced the discovery of planets around a [pulsar](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pulsar "Pulsar"), [PSR B1257+12](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/PSR_B1257%2B12 "PSR B1257+12").[[40]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-Wolszczan-40) This discovery is generally considered to be the first definitive detection of a planetary system around another star. Then, on 6 October 1995, [Michel Mayor](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Michel_Mayor "Michel Mayor") and [Didier Queloz](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Didier_Queloz "Didier Queloz") of the [Geneva Observatory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geneva_Observatory "Geneva Observatory") announced the first definitive detection of an exoplanet orbiting an ordinary [main-sequence](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Main_sequence "Main sequence") star ([51 Pegasi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/51_Pegasi "51 Pegasi")).[[214]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-Mayor-217)

The discovery of exoplanets led to another ambiguity in defining a planet: the point at which a planet becomes a star. Many known exoplanets are many times the mass of Jupiter, approaching that of stellar objects known as [brown dwarfs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Brown_dwarf "Brown dwarf"). Brown dwarfs are generally considered stars due to their theoretical ability to fuse [deuterium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Deuterium "Deuterium"), a heavier isotope of [hydrogen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hydrogen "Hydrogen"). Although objects more massive than 75 times that of Jupiter fuse simple hydrogen, objects of 13 Jupiter masses can fuse deuterium. Deuterium is quite rare, constituting less than 0.0026% of the hydrogen in the galaxy, and most brown dwarfs would have ceased fusing deuterium long before their discovery, making them effectively indistinguishable from supermassive planets.[[215]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-218)

#### IAU working definition of exoplanets

The 2006 IAU definition presents some challenges for exoplanets because the language is specific to the Solar System and the criteria of roundness and orbital zone clearance are not presently observable for exoplanets.[[1]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-exodef-1) In 2018, this definition was reassessed and updated as knowledge of exoplanets increased.[[216]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-iauexo-219) The current official working definition of an exoplanet is as follows:[[99]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-exoworkdef-100)

> 1.   Objects with true masses below the limiting mass for thermonuclear fusion of deuterium (currently calculated to be 13 Jupiter masses for objects of solar metallicity) that orbit stars, brown dwarfs, or stellar remnants and that have a mass ratio with the central object below the [L4/L5 instability](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lagrange_point#Stability "Lagrange point") (M/M central< 2/(25+√621) are "planets" (no matter how they formed). The minimum mass/size required for an extrasolar object to be considered a planet should be the same as that used in our Solar System.
> 2.   Substellar objects with true masses above the limiting mass for thermonuclear fusion of deuterium are "brown dwarfs", no matter how they formed nor where they are located.
> 3.   Free-floating objects in young star clusters with masses below the limiting mass for thermonuclear fusion of deuterium are not "planets", but are "sub-brown dwarfs" (or whatever name is most appropriate).[[99]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-exoworkdef-100)

The IAU noted that this definition could be expected to evolve as knowledge improves.[[99]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-exoworkdef-100) A 2022 review article discussing the history and rationale of this definition suggested that the words "in young star clusters" should be deleted in clause 3, as such objects have now been found elsewhere, and that the term "sub-brown dwarfs" should be replaced by the more current "free-floating planetary mass objects". The term "[planetary mass object](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planetary-mass_object "Planetary-mass object")" has also been used to refer to ambiguous situations concerning exoplanets, such as objects with mass typical for a planet that are free-floating or orbit a brown dwarf instead of a star.[[216]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-iauexo-219) Free-floating objects of planetary mass have sometimes been called planets anyway, specifically [rogue planets](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rogue_planet "Rogue planet").[[217]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-eso2120-220)

The limit of 13 Jupiter masses is not universally accepted. Objects below this mass limit can sometimes burn deuterium, and the amount of deuterium that is burned depends on an object's composition.[[218]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-bodenheimer2013-221)[[219]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-222) Furthermore, deuterium is quite scarce, so the stage of deuterium burning does not actually last very long; unlike hydrogen burning in a star, deuterium burning does not significantly affect the future evolution of an object.[[58]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-Hatzes-59) The relationship between mass and radius (or density) show no special feature at this limit, according to which brown dwarfs have the same physics and internal structure as lighter Jovian planets, and would more naturally be considered planets.[[58]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-Hatzes-59)[[54]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-ChenKipping-55)

Thus, many catalogues of exoplanets include objects heavier than 13 Jupiter masses, sometimes going up to 60 Jupiter masses.[[220]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-223)[[100]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-corot-101)[[101]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-eod-102)[[221]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-224) (The limit for hydrogen burning and becoming a [red dwarf](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Red_dwarf "Red dwarf") star is about 80 Jupiter masses.)[[58]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-Hatzes-59) The situation of main-sequence stars has been used to argue for such an inclusive definition of "planet" as well, as they also differ greatly along the two orders of magnitude that they cover, in their structure, atmospheres, temperature, spectral features, and probably formation mechanisms; yet they are all considered as one class, being all hydrostatic-equilibrium objects undergoing nuclear burning.[[58]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-Hatzes-59)

Mythology and naming
--------------------

### Classical planets

The names for the planets of the [Solar System](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_System "Solar System") (other than [Earth](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth "Earth")) in the [English language](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/English_language "English language") are derived from naming practices developed consecutively by the [Babylonians](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Babylonians "Babylonians"), [Greeks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ancient_Greece "Ancient Greece"), and [Romans](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_people "Roman people") of [antiquity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Classical_antiquity "Classical antiquity"). The practice of grafting the names of gods onto the planets was almost certainly borrowed from the Babylonians by the ancient Greeks, and thereafter from the Greeks by the Romans. The Babylonians named Venus after the [Sumerian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sumer "Sumer") goddess of love with the [Akkadian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Akkadian_language "Akkadian language") name [Ishtar](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ishtar "Ishtar"); Mars after their god of war, [Nergal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nergal "Nergal"); Mercury after their god of wisdom [Nabu](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nabu "Nabu"); Jupiter after their chief god, [Marduk](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Marduk "Marduk"); and Saturn after their god of farming, [Ninurta](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ninurta "Ninurta").[[222]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-225) There are too many concordances between Greek and Babylonian naming conventions for them to have arisen separately.[[164]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-practice-165) Given the differences in mythology, the correspondence was not perfect. For instance, the Babylonian Nergal was a god of war, and thus the Greeks identified him with Ares. Unlike Ares, Nergal was also a god of pestilence and ruler of the underworld.[[223]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-226)[[224]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-227)[[225]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-228)

In ancient Greece, the two great luminaries, the Sun and the Moon, were called _[Helios](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Helios "Helios")_ and _[Selene](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Selene "Selene")_, two ancient [Titanic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Titan_(mythology) "Titan (mythology)") deities; the slowest planet, Saturn, was called _[Phainon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Phaenon "Phaenon")_, the shiner; followed by _[Phaethon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Phaethon "Phaethon")_, Jupiter, "bright"; the red planet, Mars was known as _[Pyroeis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pyroeis "Pyroeis")_, the "fiery"; the brightest, Venus, was known as _[Phosphoros](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Phosphorus\_(morning\_star) "Phosphorus (morning star)")_, the light bringer; and the fleeting final planet, Mercury, was called _[Stilbon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stilbon\_(mythology) "Stilbon (mythology)")_, the gleamer. The Greeks assigned each planet to one among their pantheon of gods, the [Olympians](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Twelve_Olympians "Twelve Olympians") and the earlier Titans:[[164]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-practice-165)

*   Helios and Selene were the names of both planets and gods, both of them Titans (later supplanted by Olympians [Apollo](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Apollo "Apollo") and [Artemis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis "Artemis"));
*   Phainon was sacred to [Cronus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cronus "Cronus"), the Titan who fathered the Olympians, associated with the harvest;
*   Phaethon was sacred to [Zeus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zeus "Zeus"), Cronus's son who deposed him as king;
*   Pyroeis was given to [Ares](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ares "Ares"), son of Zeus and god of war;
*   Phosphoros was ruled by [Aphrodite](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aphrodite "Aphrodite"), the goddess of love; and
*   Stilbon with its speedy motion, was ruled over by [Hermes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hermes "Hermes"), messenger of the gods and god of learning and wit.[[164]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-practice-165)

[![Image 19](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Olympians.jpg/250px-Olympians.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Olympians.jpg)

The Greek gods of [Olympus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mount_Olympus "Mount Olympus"), after whom the [Solar System](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_System "Solar System")'s Roman names of the planets are derived

Although modern Greeks still use their ancient names for the planets, other European languages, because of the influence of the [Roman Empire](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Empire "Roman Empire") and, later, the [Catholic Church](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Catholic_Church "Catholic Church"), use the Roman (Latin) names rather than the Greek ones. The Romans inherited [Proto-Indo-European mythology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Proto-Indo-European_mythology "Proto-Indo-European mythology") as the Greeks did and shared with them a [common pantheon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_mythology "Roman mythology") under different names, but the Romans lacked the rich narrative traditions that Greek poetic culture had given [their gods](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Greek_mythology "Greek mythology"). During the later period of the [Roman Republic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Republic "Roman Republic"), Roman writers borrowed much of the Greek narratives and applied them to their own pantheon, to the point where they became virtually indistinguishable.[[226]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-229) When the Romans studied Greek astronomy, they gave the planets their own gods' names: _[Mercurius](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mercury\_(mythology) "Mercury (mythology)")_ (for Hermes), _[Venus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Venus\_(mythology) "Venus (mythology)")_ (Aphrodite), _[Mars](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars\_(mythology) "Mars (mythology)")_ (Ares), _[Iuppiter](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jupiter\_(mythology) "Jupiter (mythology)")_ (Zeus), and _[Saturnus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Saturn\_(mythology) "Saturn (mythology)")_ (Cronus). However, there was not much agreement on which god a particular planet was associated with; according to [Pliny the Elder](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder "Pliny the Elder"), while Phainon and Phaethon's associations with Saturn and Jupiter respectively were widely agreed upon, Pyroeis was also associated with the demi-god [Hercules](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hercules "Hercules"), Stilbon was also associated with [Apollo](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Apollo "Apollo"), god of music, healing, and prophecy; Phosphoros was also associated with prominent goddesses [Juno](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Juno_(mythology) "Juno (mythology)") and [Isis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Isis "Isis").[[227]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-DRGA_Planetae-230) Some Romans, following a belief possibly originating in [Mesopotamia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mesopotamia "Mesopotamia") but developed in [Hellenistic Egypt](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hellenistic_Egypt "Hellenistic Egypt"), believed that the seven gods after whom the planets were named took hourly shifts in looking after affairs on Earth. The order of shifts went Saturn, Jupiter, Mars, Sun, Venus, Mercury, Moon (from the farthest to the closest planet).[[228]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-zerubavel-231) Therefore, the first day was started by Saturn (1st hour), second day by Sun (25th hour), followed by Moon (49th hour), Mars, Mercury, Jupiter, and Venus. Because each day was named by the god that started it, this became the order of the [days of the week](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Week-day_names "Week-day names") in the [Roman calendar](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_calendar "Roman calendar").[[229]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-weekdays-232) In English, _Saturday_, _Sunday_, and _Monday_ are straightforward translations of these Roman names. The other days were renamed after _[Tīw](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/T%C3%BDr "Týr")_ (Tuesday), _[Wōden](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Woden "Woden")_ (Wednesday), _[Þunor](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thor "Thor")_ (Thursday), and _[Frīġ](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Frige "Frige")_ (Friday), the [Anglo-Saxon gods](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anglo-Saxon_gods "Anglo-Saxon gods") considered similar or equivalent to Mars, Mercury, Jupiter, and Venus, respectively.[[230]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-233)

Earth's name in English is not derived from Greco-Roman mythology. Because it was only generally accepted as a planet in the 17th century,[[181]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-galileo_project-182) there is no tradition of naming it after a god. (The same is true, in English at least, of the Sun and the Moon, though they are no longer generally considered planets.) The name originates from the [Old English](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Old_English "Old English") word _eorþe_, which was the word for "ground" and "dirt" as well as the world itself.[[231]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-234) As with its equivalents in the other [Germanic languages](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Germanic_languages "Germanic languages"), it derives ultimately from the [Proto-Germanic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Proto-Germanic "Proto-Germanic") word _erþō_, as can be seen in the English _earth_, the German _Erde_, the Dutch _aarde_, and the Scandinavian _jord_. Many of the [Romance languages](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Romance_languages "Romance languages") retain the old Roman word _[terra](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Terra\_(mythology) "Terra (mythology)")_ (or some variation of it) that was used with the meaning of "dry land" as opposed to "sea".[[232]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-235) The non-Romance languages use their own native words. The Greeks retain their original name, _[Γή](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gaia\_(mythology) "Gaia (mythology)")_ _(Ge)_.[[233]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-236)

Non-European cultures use other planetary-naming systems. [India](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/India "India") uses a system based on the [Navagraha](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Navagraha "Navagraha"), which incorporates the seven traditional planets and the ascending and descending [lunar nodes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lunar_node "Lunar node")_[Rahu](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rahu "Rahu")_ and [_Ketu_](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ketu_(mythology) "Ketu (mythology)"). The planets are _[Surya](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Surya "Surya")_ 'Sun', _[Chandra](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chandra "Chandra")_ 'Moon', _[Budha](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Budha "Budha")_ for Mercury, _[Shukra](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shukra "Shukra")_ ('bright') for Venus, _[Mangala](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mangala "Mangala")_ (the god of war) for Mars, _[\_Bṛhaspati\_](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/B%E1%B9%9Bhaspati "Bṛhaspati")_ (councilor of the gods) for Jupiter, and _[Shani](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shani "Shani")_ (symbolic of time) for Saturn.[[234]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-237)

The native [Persian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Persian_language "Persian language") names of most of the planets are based on identifications of the Mesopotamian gods with Iranian gods, analogous to the Greek and Latin names. Mercury is _Tir_ (Persian: تیر) for the western Iranian god Tīriya (patron of scribes), analogous to Nabu; Venus is _Nāhid_ (ناهید) for [Anahita](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anahita "Anahita"); Mars is _Bahrām_ (بهرام) for [Verethragna](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Verethragna "Verethragna"); and Jupiter is _Hormoz_ (هرمز) for [Ahura Mazda](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ahura_Mazda "Ahura Mazda"). The Persian name for Saturn, _Keyvān_ (کیوان), is a borrowing from [Akkadian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Akkadian_language "Akkadian language")_kajamānu_, meaning "the permanent, steady".[[235]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-238)

China and the countries of eastern Asia historically subject to [Chinese cultural influence](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sinosphere "Sinosphere") (such as [Japan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Japan "Japan"), [Korea](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Korea "Korea"), and [Vietnam](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vietnam "Vietnam")) use a naming system based on the [five Chinese elements](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wuxing_(Chinese_philosophy) "Wuxing (Chinese philosophy)"): [water](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Water_(wuxing) "Water (wuxing)") (Mercury [水](https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E6%B0%B4 "wikt:水")星 "water star"), [metal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Metal_(wuxing) "Metal (wuxing)") or gold (Venus [金](https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E9%87%91 "wikt:金")星 "gold star"), [fire](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fire_(wuxing) "Fire (wuxing)") (Mars [火](https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E7%81%AB "wikt:火")星 "fire star"), [wood](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wood_(wuxing) "Wood (wuxing)") (Jupiter [木](https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E6%9C%A8 "wikt:木")星 "wood star"), and [earth](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth_(wuxing) "Earth (wuxing)") or soil (Saturn [土](https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E5%9C%9F "wikt:土")星 "soil star").[[229]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-weekdays-232)

In traditional [Hebrew astronomy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hebrew_astronomy "Hebrew astronomy"), the seven traditional planets have (for the most part) descriptive names—the Sun is חמה _Ḥammah_ or "the hot one", the Moon is לבנה _Levanah_ or "the white one", Venus is כוכב נוגה _Kokhav Nogah_ or "the bright planet", Mercury is כוכב _Kokhav_ or "the planet" (given its lack of distinguishing features), Mars is מאדים _Ma'adim_ or "the red one", and Saturn is שבתאי _Shabbatai_ or "the resting one" (in reference to its slow movement compared to the other visible planets).[[236]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-Hebrew-239) The odd one out is Jupiter, called צדק _Tzedeq_ or "justice".[[236]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-Hebrew-239) These names, first attested in the [Babylonian Talmud](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Babylonian_Talmud "Babylonian Talmud"), are not the original Hebrew names of the planets. In 377 [Epiphanius of Salamis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Epiphanius_of_Salamis "Epiphanius of Salamis") recorded another set of names that seem to have pagan or [Canaanite](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Canaanite_religion "Canaanite religion") associations: those names, since replaced for religious reasons, were probably the historical Semitic names, and may have much earlier roots going back to Babylonian astronomy.[[236]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-Hebrew-239) The etymologies for the Arabic names of the planets are less well understood. Mostly agreed among scholars are Venus (Arabic: الزهرة, _az-Zuhara_, "the bright one"[[237]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-240)), Earth (الأرض, _al-ʾArḍ_, from the same root as [eretz](https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%D7%90%D7%A8%D7%A5 "wikt:ארץ")), and Saturn (زُحَل, _Zuḥal_, "withdrawer"[[238]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-241)). Multiple suggested etymologies exist for Mercury (عُطَارِد, _ʿUṭārid_), Mars (اَلْمِرِّيخ, _al-Mirrīkh_), and Jupiter (المشتري, _al-Muštarī_), but there is no agreement among scholars.[[239]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-242)[[240]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-243)[[241]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-244)[[242]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-245)

### Modern discoveries

When subsequent planets were discovered in the 18th and 19th centuries, Uranus was named for a [Greek deity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Uranus_(mythology) "Uranus (mythology)") and Neptune for a [Roman one](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neptune_(mythology) "Neptune (mythology)") (the counterpart of [Poseidon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Poseidon "Poseidon")). The asteroids were initially named from mythology as well—[Ceres](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ceres_(mythology) "Ceres (mythology)"), [Juno](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Juno_(mythology) "Juno (mythology)"), and [Vesta](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vesta_(mythology) "Vesta (mythology)") are major Roman goddesses, and Pallas is an epithet of the major Greek goddess [Athena](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Athena_(mythology) "Athena (mythology)")—but as more and more were discovered, they first started being named after more minor goddesses, and the mythological restriction was dropped starting from the twentieth asteroid [Massalia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/20_Massalia "20 Massalia") in 1852.[[243]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-246) Pluto (named after the [Greek god of the underworld](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pluto_(mythology) "Pluto (mythology)")) was given a classical name, as it was considered a major planet when it was discovered.[_[citation needed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation\_needed "Wikipedia:Citation needed")_]

The names of Uranus ([天](https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E5%A4%A9 "wikt:天")王星 "sky king star"), Neptune ([海](https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E6%B5%B7 "wikt:海")王星 "sea king star"), and Pluto ([冥](https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E5%86%A5 "wikt:冥")王星 "underworld king star") in Chinese, Korean, and Japanese are [calques](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Calque "Calque") based on the roles of those gods in Roman and Greek mythology.[[244]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-bianyulin-247)[[245]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-248)[[d]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-250) In the 19th century, [Alexander Wylie](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_Wylie_(missionary) "Alexander Wylie (missionary)") and [Li Shanlan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Li_Shanlan "Li Shanlan") calqued the names of the first 117 asteroids into Chinese, and many of their names are still used today, e.g. Ceres ([穀](https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E7%A9%80 "wikt:穀")神星 "grain goddess star"), Pallas ([智](https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E6%99%BA "wikt:智")神星 "wisdom goddess star"), Juno ([婚](https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E5%A9%9A "wikt:婚")神星 "marriage goddess star"), Vesta ([灶](https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E7%81%B6 "wikt:灶")神星 "hearth goddess star"), and Hygiea ([健](https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E5%81%A5 "wikt:健")神星 "health goddess star").[[247]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-lijing-251) Such translations were extended to some later minor planets, including some of the dwarf planets discovered in the 21st century, e.g. Haumea ([妊](https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E5%A6%8A "wikt:妊")神星 "pregnancy goddess star"), Makemake ([鳥](https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E9%B3%A5 "wikt:鳥")神星 "bird goddess star"), and Eris ([鬩](https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E9%AC%A9 "wikt:鬩")神星 "quarrel goddess star"). However, except for the better-known asteroids and dwarf planets, many of them are rare outside Chinese astronomical dictionaries.[[244]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-bianyulin-247)

Hebrew names were chosen for Uranus (אורון _Oron_, "small light") and Neptune (רהב _Rahab_, a Biblical sea monster) in 2009;[[248]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-252) prior to that the names "Uranus" and "Neptune" had simply been borrowed.[[249]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-253)

After more objects were discovered beyond Neptune, naming conventions depending on their orbits were put in place: those in the 2:3 resonance with Neptune (the [plutinos](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Plutino "Plutino")) are given names from underworld myths, while others are given names from creation myths. Most of the trans-Neptunian planetoids are named after gods and goddesses from other cultures (e.g. [Quaoar](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/50000_Quaoar "50000 Quaoar") is named after a [Tongva](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tongva "Tongva") god). There are a few exceptions which continue the Roman and Greek scheme, notably including Eris as it had initially been considered a tenth planet.[[250]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-WGSBN-Naming-Guidelines-254)[[251]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-WGSBN-home-255)

The moons (including the planetary-mass ones) are generally given names with some association with their parent planet. The planetary-mass moons of Jupiter are named after four of Zeus' lovers (or other sexual partners); those of Saturn are named after Cronus' brothers and sisters, the Titans; those of Uranus are named after characters from [Shakespeare](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/William_Shakespeare "William Shakespeare") and [Pope](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_Pope "Alexander Pope") (originally specifically from fairy mythology,[[252]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-Lassell_1852-256) but that ended with the naming of [Miranda](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Miranda_(moon) "Miranda (moon)")). Neptune's planetary-mass moon Triton is named after [the god's son](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Triton_(mythology) "Triton (mythology)"); Pluto's planetary-mass moon Charon is named after the [ferryman of the dead](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Charon "Charon"), who carries the souls of the newly deceased to the underworld (Pluto's domain).[[253]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-257)

### Exoplanets

Exoplanets are commonly named after their parent star and their order of discovery within its planetary system, such as [Proxima Centauri b](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Proxima_Centauri_b "Proxima Centauri b"). (The lettering starts at b, with a considered to represent the parent star.)[_[citation needed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation\_needed "Wikipedia:Citation needed")_]

### Symbols

Most common planetary symbols Sun 

[![Image 20: ☉](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Sun_symbol_%28fixed_width%29.svg/14px-Sun_symbol_%28fixed_width%29.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Sun_symbol_(fixed_width).svg "☉")Mercury 

[![Image 21: ☿](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Mercury_symbol_%28fixed_width%29.svg/14px-Mercury_symbol_%28fixed_width%29.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Mercury_symbol_(fixed_width).svg "☿")Venus 

[![Image 22: ♀](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Venus_symbol_%28fixed_width%29.svg/14px-Venus_symbol_%28fixed_width%29.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Venus_symbol_(fixed_width).svg "♀")Earth 

[![Image 23: 🜨](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Earth_symbol_%28fixed_width%29.svg/14px-Earth_symbol_%28fixed_width%29.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Earth_symbol_(fixed_width).svg "🜨")Moon 

[![Image 24: ☾](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Moon_decrescent_symbol.svg/14px-Moon_decrescent_symbol.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Moon_decrescent_symbol.svg "☾")Mars 

[![Image 25: ♂](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Mars_symbol_%28fixed_width%29.svg/14px-Mars_symbol_%28fixed_width%29.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Mars_symbol_(fixed_width).svg "♂")Jupiter 

[![Image 26: ♃](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Jupiter_symbol_%28fixed_width%29.svg/14px-Jupiter_symbol_%28fixed_width%29.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Jupiter_symbol_(fixed_width).svg "♃")Saturn 

[![Image 27: ♄](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Saturn_symbol_%28fixed_width%29.svg/14px-Saturn_symbol_%28fixed_width%29.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Saturn_symbol_(fixed_width).svg "♄")Uranus 

[![Image 28: ⛢](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Uranus_symbol_%28fixed_width%29.svg/14px-Uranus_symbol_%28fixed_width%29.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Uranus_symbol_(fixed_width).svg "⛢") or [![Image 29: ♅](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Uranus_monogram_%28fixed_width%29.svg/14px-Uranus_monogram_%28fixed_width%29.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Uranus_monogram_(fixed_width).svg "♅")Neptune 

[![Image 30: ♆](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Neptune_symbol_%28fixed_width%29.svg/14px-Neptune_symbol_%28fixed_width%29.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Neptune_symbol_(fixed_width).svg "♆")

The written symbols for Mercury, Venus, Jupiter, Saturn, and possibly Mars have been traced to forms found in late Greek papyrus texts.[[254]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-jones-1999-258) The symbols for Jupiter and Saturn are identified as [monograms](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Monogram "Monogram") of the corresponding Greek names, and the symbol for Mercury is a stylized [caduceus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Caduceus "Caduceus").[[254]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-jones-1999-258)

According to [Annie Scott Dill Maunder](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Annie_Scott_Dill_Maunder "Annie Scott Dill Maunder"), antecedents of the planetary symbols were used in art to represent the gods associated with the classical planets. _Bianchini's [planisphere](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planisphere "Planisphere")_, discovered by Francesco Bianchini in the 18th century but produced in the 2nd century,[[255]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-imss-259) shows Greek personifications of planetary gods charged with early versions of the planetary symbols. Mercury has a [caduceus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Caduceus "Caduceus"); Venus has, attached to her necklace, a cord connected to another necklace; Mars, a spear; Jupiter, a staff; Saturn, a scythe; the [Sun](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun "Sun"), a [circlet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Circlet "Circlet") with rays radiating from it; and the Moon, a headdress with a crescent attached.[[256]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-maunder-260) The modern shapes with the cross-marks first appeared around the 16th century. According to Maunder, the addition of crosses appears to be "an attempt to give a savour of Christianity to the symbols of the old pagan gods."[[256]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-maunder-260) Earth itself was not considered a classical planet; its symbol descends from a pre-heliocentric symbol for the [four corners of the world](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Four_corners_of_the_world "Four corners of the world").[[257]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-high-school-astronomy-261)

When further planets were discovered orbiting the Sun, symbols were invented for them. The most common astronomical symbol for Uranus, ⛢,[[258]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-iancu-262) was invented by [Johann Gottfried Köhler](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Johann_Gottfried_K%C3%B6hler "Johann Gottfried Köhler"), and was intended to represent the newly discovered metal [platinum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Platinum "Platinum").[[259]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-bode-uranus-263)[[260]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-gould-uranus-264) An alternative symbol, ♅, was invented by [Jérôme Lalande](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/J%C3%A9r%C3%B4me_Lalande "Jérôme Lalande"), and represents a globe with a H on top, for Uranus's discoverer Herschel.[[261]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-Francisca-265) Today, ⛢ is mostly used by astronomers and ♅ by [astrologers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Astrology "Astrology"), though it is possible to find each symbol in the other context.[[258]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-iancu-262) The first few asteroids were considered to be planets when they were discovered, and were likewise given abstract symbols, e.g. Ceres' sickle (⚳), Pallas' spear (⚴), Juno's sceptre (⚵), and Vesta's hearth (⚶). However, as their number rose further and further, this practice stopped in favour of numbering them instead. (Massalia, the first asteroid not named from mythology, is also the first asteroid that was not assigned a symbol by its discoverer.) The symbols for the first four asteroids, Ceres through Vesta, remained in use for longer than the others,[[153]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-asteroids-154) and even in the modern day [NASA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NASA "NASA") has used the Ceres symbol—Ceres being the only asteroid that is also a dwarf planet.[[262]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-miller-266) Neptune's symbol (♆) represents [the god's trident](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neptune%27s_trident "Neptune's trident").[[260]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-gould-uranus-264) The astronomical symbol for Pluto is a P-L monogram (♇),[[263]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-JPL/NASA_Pluto's_Symbol-267) though it has become less common since the IAU definition reclassified Pluto.[[262]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-miller-266) Since Pluto's reclassification, NASA has used the traditional astrological symbol of Pluto (⯓), a planetary orb over Pluto's [bident](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bident "Bident").[[262]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-miller-266)

Some rarer planetary symbols in Unicode Earth 

[![Image 31: ♁](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Globus_cruciger_%28fixed_width%29.svg/14px-Globus_cruciger_%28fixed_width%29.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Globus_cruciger_(fixed_width).svg "♁")Vesta 

[![Image 32: ⚶](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Vesta_symbol_%28fixed_width%29.svg/14px-Vesta_symbol_%28fixed_width%29.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Vesta_symbol_(fixed_width).svg "⚶")Juno 

[![Image 33: ⚵](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Juno_symbol_%28fixed_width%29.svg/14px-Juno_symbol_%28fixed_width%29.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Juno_symbol_(fixed_width).svg "⚵")Ceres 

[![Image 34: ⚳](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Ceres_symbol_%28fixed_width%29.svg/14px-Ceres_symbol_%28fixed_width%29.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Ceres_symbol_(fixed_width).svg "⚳")Pallas 

[![Image 35: ⚴](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Pallas_symbol_%28fixed_width%29.svg/14px-Pallas_symbol_%28fixed_width%29.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Pallas_symbol_(fixed_width).svg "⚴")Hygiea 

[![Image 36: ⯚](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Hygiea_symbol_%28fixed_width%29.svg/14px-Hygiea_symbol_%28fixed_width%29.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Hygiea_symbol_(fixed_width).svg "⯚")Orcus 

[![Image 37: 🝿](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Orcus_symbol_%28fixed_width%29.svg/14px-Orcus_symbol_%28fixed_width%29.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Orcus_symbol_(fixed_width).svg "🝿")Pluto 

[![Image 38: ♇](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Pluto_monogram_%28fixed_width%29.svg/14px-Pluto_monogram_%28fixed_width%29.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Pluto_monogram_(fixed_width).svg "♇") or [![Image 39: ⯓](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Pluto_symbol_%28large_orb%2C_fixed_width%29.svg/14px-Pluto_symbol_%28large_orb%2C_fixed_width%29.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Pluto_symbol_(large_orb,_fixed_width).svg "⯓")Charon 

[![Image 40: ⯕](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Charon_symbol_%28fixed_width%29.svg/14px-Charon_symbol_%28fixed_width%29.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Charon_symbol_(fixed_width).svg "⯕")Haumea 

[![Image 41: 🝻](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Haumea_symbol_%28fixed_width%29.svg/14px-Haumea_symbol_%28fixed_width%29.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Haumea_symbol_(fixed_width).svg "🝻")Quaoar 

[![Image 42: 🝾](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Quaoar_symbol_%28fixed_width%29.svg/14px-Quaoar_symbol_%28fixed_width%29.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Quaoar_symbol_(fixed_width).svg "🝾")Makemake 

[![Image 43: 🝼](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Makemake_symbol_%28fixed_width%29.svg/14px-Makemake_symbol_%28fixed_width%29.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Makemake_symbol_(fixed_width).svg "🝼")Gonggong 

[![Image 44: 🝽](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Gonggong_symbol_%28fixed_width%29.svg/14px-Gonggong_symbol_%28fixed_width%29.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Gonggong_symbol_(fixed_width).svg "🝽")Eris 

[![Image 45: ⯰](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Eris_symbol_%28fixed_width%29.svg/14px-Eris_symbol_%28fixed_width%29.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Eris_symbol_(fixed_width).svg "⯰")Sedna 

[![Image 46: ⯲](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Sedna_symbol_%28fixed_width%29.svg/14px-Sedna_symbol_%28fixed_width%29.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Sedna_symbol_(fixed_width).svg "⯲")

The IAU discourages the use of planetary symbols in modern journal articles in favour of one-letter or (to disambiguate Mercury and Mars) two-letter abbreviations for the major planets. The symbols for the Sun and Earth are nonetheless common, as [solar mass](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_mass "Solar mass"), [Earth mass](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth_mass "Earth mass"), and similar units are common in astronomy.[[264]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-iau-style-manual-268) Other planetary symbols today are mostly encountered in astrology. Astrologers have resurrected the old astronomical symbols for the first few asteroids and continue to invent symbols for other objects.[[262]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-miller-266) This includes relatively standard astrological symbols for the dwarf planets discovered in the 21st century, which were not given symbols by astronomers because planetary symbols had mostly fallen out of use in astronomy by the time they were discovered. Many astrological symbols are included in [Unicode](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Unicode "Unicode"), and a few of these new inventions (the symbols of Haumea, Makemake, and Eris) have since been used by NASA in astronomy.[[262]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-miller-266) The Eris symbol is a traditional one from [Discordianism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Discordianism "Discordianism"), a religion worshipping the goddess Eris. The other dwarf-planet symbols are mostly initialisms (except Haumea) in the native scripts of the cultures they come from; they also represent something associated with the corresponding deity or culture, e.g. Makemake's face or Gonggong's snake-tail.[[262]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-miller-266)[[265]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-269) Moskowitz also devised symbols for the planetary-mass moons; most of them are initialisms combined with a feature of their parent planet's symbol. The exception is Charon, which combines the high orb of Pluto's bident symbol with a crescent, suggesting both Charon as a moon and the mythological Charon's boat crossing the river [Styx](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Styx "Styx").[[266]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-270)

--------

*   [Double planet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Double_planet "Double planet")– Binary system where two planetary-mass objects share an orbital axis external to both
*   [List of landings on extraterrestrial bodies](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_landings_on_extraterrestrial_bodies "List of landings on extraterrestrial bodies")
*   [Lists of planets](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lists_of_planets "Lists of planets") – A list of lists of planets sorted by diverse attributes
*   [Mesoplanet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mesoplanet "Mesoplanet")– Planetary objects that have a mass smaller than Mercury but larger than Ceres
*   [Planetary habitability](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planetary_habitability "Planetary habitability")– Known extent to which a planet is suitable for life
*   [Planetary mnemonic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planetary_mnemonic "Planetary mnemonic")– Phrase used to remember the planets of the Solar System
*   [Theoretical planetology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Theoretical_planetology "Theoretical planetology")– Scientific modeling of planets

Data for [G-type stars](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/G-type_star "G-type star") like the Sun is not available. This statistic is an extrapolation from data on [K-type stars](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/K-type_star "K-type star").[[51]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-ucb1in5-51)[[52]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_note-earthsunhz-52)

In Vietnamese, [calques](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Calque "Calque") are more common than directly reading these names as [Sino-Vietnamese](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sino-Vietnamese_vocabulary "Sino-Vietnamese vocabulary"), e.g. _sao Thuỷ_ rather than _Thuỷ tinh_ for Mercury. 172.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet#cite_ref-Cooley_173-0)**Cooley, Jeffrey L. (2008). ["Inana and Šukaletuda: A Sumerian Astral Myth"](https://www.academia.edu/1247599). _KASKAL_. **5**: 161–172. [ISSN](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ISSN_(identifier) "ISSN (identifier)")[1971-8608](https://search.worldcat.org/issn/1971-8608). [Archived](https://web.archive.org/web/20191224105634/https://www.academia.edu/1247599) from the original on 24 December 2019. Retrieved 26 November 2022. The Greeks, for example, originally identified the morning and evening stars with two separate deities, Phosphoros and Hesporos respectively. In Mesopotamia, it seems that this was recognized prehistorically. Assuming its authenticity, a cylinder seal from the Erlenmeyer collection attests to this knowledge in southern Iraq as early as the Late Uruk / Jemdet Nasr Period, as do the archaic texts of the period. [...] Whether or not one accepts the seal as authentic, the fact that there is no epithetical distinction between the morning and evening appearances of Venus in any later Mesopotamian literature attests to a very, very early recognition of the phenomenon.
